limitation of VscAi

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-11},
"Incorrect output. Models can generate code that looks correct but contains bugs, uses deprecated APIs, or doesn't handle edge cases. Always test AI-generated code, especially for logic that affects security, data integrity, or critical flows.
Prompt injection. Malicious content in files, tool outputs, or web pages can attempt to redirect the agent's behavior. This is why VS Code includes tool approval gates and trust boundaries. Learn more about AI security.
Treat AI-generated output as a first draft: useful as a starting point, but always requiring your review and judgment. For more on how models work, including nondeterminism, knowledge boundaries, and context limits, see Language models."
[{2026-04-11 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/trust-and-safety#_ai-limitations-to-watch-for]

Chat of VscAi

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-10},
· Chat in Visual Studio Code enables you to use natural language for AI-powered coding assistance.

"Chat: the primary interface for interacting with agents and having multi-turn conversations. Use chat to assign tasks, ask questions, explore ideas, or get explanations. Switch between Agent, Ask, Plan and custom agents depending on your goal.
The VS Code Chat experience (primarily powered by **GitHub Copilot**) has evolved from a simple sidebar into a multi-layered "AI Agent" system. As of early 2026, it is no longer just a window for questions; it is an active participant in your development workflow.
Here is a high-level overview of how Chat is structured and its key capabilities.
---
## 1. The Three Layers of Chat
VS Code provides three ways to interact with AI, depending on your "zone" of work:
* **Chat View (`Ctrl+Alt+I`):** The persistent sidebar for deep-dives, brainstorming, and multi-file tasks. It maintains a long-term session history and provides the most comprehensive tools.
* **Inline Chat (`Ctrl+I`):** A lightweight bar that appears directly inside your code editor or terminal. Use this for quick refactors, explaining a specific line, or fixing a bug without losing your place.
* **Quick Chat (`Ctrl+Shift+Alt+L`):** A dropdown (similar to the Command Palette) for "one-and-done" questions while you're focused on another task.
---
## 2. Core Capabilities & "Smart Actions"
Beyond just answering questions, the Chat system can execute actions directly on your workspace:
* **Code Explanation & Refactoring:** Use `/explain` or `/fix` to analyze selected code.
* **Test Generation:** Use `/tests` to scaffold unit tests for the current file.
* **Terminal Integration:** Chat understands your terminal errors. You can use it to explain a stack trace or generate the correct CLI command to fix a build error.
* **Commit & PR Assistance:** Chat can automatically generate meaningful Git commit messages and Pull Request summaries based on your changes.
---
## 3. Agents & "Autopilot" (New for 2026)
The biggest shift in 2026 is the introduction of **Autonomous Agents**.
* **Agent Sessions:** You can delegate a task (e.g., *"Migrate this API to use the new schema"*) and the agent will plan the changes, edit multiple files, and run tests to verify its work.
* **Autopilot Mode:** In preview, agents can now approve their own actions and retry on errors without manual intervention for every step.
* **Integrated Browser:** Agents can now open a "headless" browser inside VS Code to verify UI changes, check for layout issues, or take screenshots for you to review.
---
## 4. Context & Customization
Chat is only as good as what it "sees." You can guide it using:
* **Variable Mentions (`#`):** Use `#file`, `#folder`, or `#selection` to tell the AI exactly what code to look at.
* **Custom Instructions:** You can define a `.github/copilot-instructions.md` file to tell the AI about your team’s specific coding standards or library preferences.
* **Model Selection:** You can switch between different "brains" (like GPT-5, Claude 4.5, or specialized local models) directly from the chat interface.
---
## 5. Summary Table
| Feature | Best For... | Keyboard Shortcut |
| **Chat View** | Research, complex refactors, and agents | `Ctrl+Alt+I` |
| **Inline Chat** | Quick edits and line-by-line fixes | `Ctrl+I` |
| **Agent Mode** | Multi-file tasks and autonomous work | Inside Chat View |
| **Slash Commands** | Common tasks (`/fix`, `/explain`, `/tests`) | Type `/` in any chat |
| **Variables** | Providing specific context (`#file`, `#terminal`) | Type `#` in any chat |
---
⭢ **Tip:** If you’re ever confused about what a specific agent or tool is doing, look for the **"Thinking"** block in the chat response. You can expand it to see the model's step-by-step reasoning and which files it accessed."
[{2026-04-09 retrieved} https://gemini.google.com/app/bd06be8f60ddef1a]

Chat-customizations-editor of VscChat

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-13},
"The Chat Customizations editor provides a centralized UI for creating and managing all your chat customizations in one place. The editor organizes the different customization types into separate tabs and provides an embedded code editor for editing customization files with syntax highlighting and validation.
You can create new customizations from scratch by editing the corresponding Markdown, or use AI to generate initial content based on your specific project.
To add MCP servers and agent plugins, you can browse the corresponding marketplace directly from the editor, install new items, and manage existing ones.
To open the Chat Customizations editor, select the Configure Chat (gear icon) in the Chat view or run Chat: Open Chat Customizations from the Command Palette (Ctrl+Shift+P).
You can configure customization for different agent types: local agents, Copilot CLI, and the Claude agent. Select the agent type from the dropdown at the top of the editor to view and manage customizations for that agent type."
[{2026-04-13 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/customization/overview#_chat-customizations-editor]

GitHub-Copilot of VscAi

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-09},
× https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/getting-started,
× https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/setup,
· GitHub Copilot in Visual Studio Code provides autonomous agents, inline suggestions, chat, and smart actions.
[{2026-04-14 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/reference/copilot-vscode-features]

"GitHub Copilot brings AI agents to Visual Studio Code. Describe what you want to build, and an agent plans the approach, writes the code, and verifies the result across your entire project. Choose from Copilot's built-in agents, third-party agents from providers like Anthropic and OpenAI, or your own custom agents, and run them locally, in the background, or in the cloud. For more targeted changes, inline suggestions and chat give you precise control directly in the editor."
[{2026-04-09 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/overview]

"VS Code offers AI across a spectrum of interaction surfaces, each suited to different tasks:
* Agents: autonomous sessions that follow the full agent loop, reading files, executing coordinated changes across multiple files, running commands, and iterating until the task is complete. Agents handle multi-step tasks end-to-end, from implementing features to architecture-level refactoring and framework migrations.
* Chat: the primary interface for interacting with agents and having multi-turn conversations. Use chat to assign tasks, ask questions, explore ideas, or get explanations. Switch between Agent, Ask, Plan and custom agents depending on your goal.
* Inline chat: a lightweight chat interface that opens directly in the editor for quick, focused edits.
* Inline suggestions: code suggestions that appear as ghost text while you type. These use specialized completion models and don't involve an agent loop or tools. Next edit suggestions (NES) go further by predicting where your next edit should happen.
* Smart actions: one-click AI actions integrated into your workflow, like generating commit messages or fixing diagnostics errors.
[{2026-04-10 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/overview]

In VS Code, agents use language models to reason about the best course of action, and tools to interact with the environment. With tools, agents can read files, make code changes, run terminal commands, and reach out to external services. The agent chains these actions together as needed until it accomplishes the task.

tool of VscAgent

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-25},
· Tools are the mechanism that lets the model act on your development environment. Without tools, a language model can only generate text. With tools, an agent can read files, write code, run terminal commands, search your codebase, and connect to external services.
During the agent loop, the model decides which tools to call based on the task. Each tool call produces output that becomes part of the context for the next iteration.
[{2026-04-10 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/tools]
· Use the Configure Tools button in the chat input field to enable or disable individual tools for the current request.

name::
* McsEngl.VscAgent'tool!⇒VscTool,
* McsEngl.Vscode'tool!⇒VscTool,
* McsEngl.VscTool!=Vscode'tool,

tool.SPECIFIC

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-10},
· VS Code supports three types of tools:
* Built-in tools: tools that ship with VS Code for common development tasks, like reading and writing files, running terminal commands, searching your codebase, and navigating the editor. These are available immediately without any setup.
* MCP tools: tools provided by Model Context Protocol (MCP) servers, an open standard for connecting AI models to external tools and data sources. MCP servers can run locally on your machine or be hosted remotely. Use MCP tools to connect to databases, APIs, and other external services.
* Extension tools: tools contributed by VS Code extensions through the Language Model Tools API. Extension tools integrate deeply with the editor and are available when you install the extension.

context of VscAgent

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-25},
"The context window is the total amount of information a model can process in a single request. It includes everything: the system prompt, custom instructions, conversation history, file contents, tool outputs, and your current message. Different models have different context window sizes.
When the context window fills up, VS Code automatically summarizes older parts of the conversation to make room. This means important details from early in a long conversation might be compressed or lost. You can also type /compact in the chat input to manually trigger compaction at any time. Optionally, add custom instructions after the command to guide the summary, for example /compact focus on the API design decisions."
[{2026-03-25 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/language-models]

name::
* McsEngl.VscAgent'context,

reasoning of VscAgent

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-25},
"Some language models can perform extended reasoning, also called "thinking", before producing a response. Instead of generating an answer immediately, a reasoning model first works through the problem internally, considering multiple approaches, evaluating trade-offs, and building a step-by-step chain of thought. This internal reasoning happens in dedicated thinking tokens that are separate from the final output.
Reasoning models are especially effective for complex tasks like multi-step debugging, architectural planning, code refactoring, and mathematical or scientific analysis. For simpler tasks like generating boilerplate or answering basic questions, the extra reasoning adds latency without significant benefit."
[{2026-03-25 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/language-models]

name::
* McsEngl.VscAgent'reasoning,
* McsEngl.VscAgent'thinking,

loop of VscAgent

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-08},
"When you give an agent a task, it follows an agentic loop. This pattern is common across modern AI assistants. Within VS Code, an agent is the system that plans and takes actions, and the language model generates responses that inform those actions.

At each step, the agent evaluates its progress and picks the next action. It might open a file to understand an API, make an edit, then run a command to verify the change worked. The output of each action becomes input for the next decision.
Diagram showing the agentic loop: User prompt -> Agent reasoning -> Tool calls (read files, edit code, run tests) -> Agent updates based on tool results -> Final output for user review

The agent loop typically involves three high-level stages:
Understand. The agent reads files, searches the codebase, and looks up documentation to understand what needs to change.
Act. The agent modifies code, runs terminal commands, installs dependencies, or calls external services through tools.
Validate. The agent runs tests, checks for compiler errors, and reviews its own changes. If something is wrong, it continues iterating.
The agent uses the language model to reason about the best course of action. However, without the ability to interact with the environment, the model is limited to providing generic responses. With tools, the agent issues tool calls at each step to gather information and take actions like reading files, making code changes, running terminal commands, and reaching out to external services.

The agent chains these actions together as needed until it accomplishes the task. Answering a question about your codebase might involve only a few file reads. Implementing a new feature typically loops through editing, running tests, diagnosing failures, and editing again until the tests pass.

Behind the scenes, VS Code assembles the current context into a prompt and sends it to the language model. The model responds with text, a code edit, or a tool request. When a tool runs, its output is added to the context for the next iteration, and this cycle repeats until the task is complete.

You stay in control throughout the process. Send a new message to redirect the agent, add context, or suggest a different approach."
[{2026-04-08 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/agents]

name::
* McsEngl.VscAgent'loop,

VscAgent.plan

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-11},
"For complex tasks, jumping straight into code generation can lead to incomplete implementations or wrong architectural decisions. The built-in Plan agent collaborates with you to research the task and create a detailed implementation plan before any code changes are made. This ensures requirements are understood, edge cases are identified, and you agree on the approach before the agent starts writing code.
The plan agent uses a 4-phase iterative workflow:
* Discovery: research the task using read-only tools and codebase analysis.
* Alignment: ask clarifying questions to resolve ambiguities.
* Design: draft a structured implementation plan.
* Refinement: iterate on the plan based on your feedback.
The Plan agent does not make code changes until the plan is reviewed and approved. Once approved, you can hand off the plan to the default agent or save it for further refinement."
[{2026-04-11 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/agents#_planning]

VscAgent.subagent

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-11},
"When working on complex tasks, the main agent can delegate subtasks to subagents. A subagent is an independent AI agent that performs focused work, such as researching a topic or analyzing code, and reports the results back to the main agent.
The primary benefit of subagents is context optimization. Without subagents, every file read, search result, and intermediate step during research accumulates in the main agent's context window, potentially crowding out important information. Subagents perform their work in a separate context window and return only a summary, keeping the main conversation focused on the task at hand."
[{2026-04-11 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/copilot/concepts/agents#_subagents]

* File-based instructions: guidelines in .instructions.md files that apply based on file path patterns or task descriptions. Use these when different parts of your codebase need different rules, such as React patterns for .tsx files or API conventions for your backend.

"Model Context Protocol (MCP) is an open standard for connecting the AI to external tools and data sources. Without MCP, the AI can only work with code and the terminal. MCP servers extend its reach by providing tools that query databases, call APIs, interact with cloud services, or access any other external system.
MCP servers run locally or remotely and can also provide resources, prompts, and interactive apps."

extension of Vscode

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2025-12-11},
× https://code.visualstudio.com/api,

"### Overview of VS Code Extensions
Visual Studio Code (VS Code) is a highly extensible code editor developed by Microsoft. Its **extensions** are one of its biggest strengths, allowing users to add support for new programming languages, debuggers, tools, themes, snippets, and more. Extensions plug directly into VS Code's UI and APIs, enabling customization for virtually any development workflow.
#### Key Features of Extensions
- **Extensibility**: Add languages (e.g., Python, Go), linters, formatters, AI assistants, Git tools, and UI enhancements.
- **Marketplace**: The official source is the [Visual Studio Code Marketplace](https://marketplace.visualstudio.com/vscode), with tens of thousands of free and paid extensions. Many are open-source.
- **Installation and Management**:
- Open the Extensions view (Ctrl+Shift+X or Cmd+Shift+X on macOS).
- Search, install, update, disable, or uninstall directly in VS Code.
- Built-in extensions (e.g., for JavaScript/TypeScript) come pre-installed.
- Security: Extensions from third-party publishers require trust confirmation. VS Code scans for malware.
- **Categories**: Include themes, language packs, linters/formatters, debuggers, AI tools, Git integrations, and productivity boosters.
- **Syncing**: With Settings Sync or Profiles, extensions can be shared across devices.
Extensions run in a separate process for stability and performance.
#### Popular VS Code Extensions in 2025
Here are some of the most widely recommended and highly installed extensions (based on recent developer surveys and articles). These span productivity, code quality, AI, and Git:
1. **GitHub Copilot** (Microsoft) – AI-powered code suggestions and completions. A game-changer for faster coding (paid, with free trial).
2. **Prettier - Code Formatter** – Automatic code formatting for consistent style across teams (supports JS, TS, HTML, CSS, etc.).
3. **ESLint** – Linting for JavaScript/TypeScript to catch errors and enforce standards.
4. **GitLens** – Supercharges Git with blame annotations, history visualization, and repository insights.
5. **Live Server** – Launches a local development server with live reload for web projects.
6. **Python** (Microsoft) – Full support for Python (IntelliSense, debugging, linting).
7. **IntelliCode** (Microsoft) – AI-assisted IntelliSense for context-aware completions.
8. **ES7+ React/Redux/React-Native Snippets** – Quick snippets for React development.
9. **Material Icon Theme** or **vscode-icons** – Enhances file/folder icons for better navigation.
10. **Auto Rename Tag** – Automatically renames paired HTML/XML tags.
11. **Bracket Pair Colorizer** (or built-in successor) – Colors matching brackets for readability.
12. **Path Intellisense** – Autocompletes filenames in imports/paths.
13. **Code Spell Checker** – Catches spelling errors in comments and strings.
14. **Thunder Client** – Lightweight API testing (alternative to Postman).
15. **Docker** (Microsoft) – Manage containers, images, and Compose directly in VS Code.
AI tools like Copilot dominate in 2025, with growing focus on security scanners (e.g., for IaC) and specialized extensions for frameworks like React or Kubernetes.
To get started, open the Extensions view and search for these by name. Many have millions of installs and high ratings. If you're new, start with Prettier, ESLint, and GitLens for immediate productivity gains!"
[{2025-12-11 retrieved} https://x.com/i/grok?conversation=1999022421228106144]

creating of VscExtn

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-30},
"## Option 2: Create a Simple Extension
Create a more powerful command that works exactly like your jEdit macro:
1. Create a new folder and open it in VS Code
2. Create `package.json`: 

json
    {
      "name": "insert-date",
      "displayName": "Insert Date",
      "description": "Insert Date as {2026-04-03}",
      "version": "0.0.1",
      "date": "{2026-04-03}"
      "publisher": "local",
      "engines": {
        "vscode": "^1.74.0"
      },
      "activationEvents": [],
      "main": "./extension.js",
      "contributes": {
        "commands": [
          {
            "command": "extension.insertDate",
            "title": "Insert Date in Braces"
          }
        ],
        "keybindings": [
          {
            "command": "extension.insertDate",
            "key": "ctrl+shift+d",
            "mac": "cmd+shift+d"
          }
        ]
      }
    }


3. Create `extension.js`: 
javascript
    const vscode = require('vscode');

    function activate(context) {
      let disposable = vscode.commands.registerCommand('insert-date.insertDate', () => {
        const editor = vscode.window.activeTextEditor;
        if (!editor) return;

        const now = new Date();
        const year = now.getFullYear();
        const month = now.getMonth() + 1;
        const day = now.getDate();

        // Format with leading zeros
        const formattedMonth = month < 10 ? '0' + month : '' + month;
        const formattedDay = day < 10 ? '0' + day : '' + day;

        const dateString = `{${year}-${formattedMonth}-${formattedDay} }`;

        editor.edit(editBuilder => {
          editBuilder.replace(editor.selection, dateString);
        });
      });

      context.subscriptions.push(disposable);
    }

    function deactivate() {}

    module.exports = {
      activate,
      deactivate
    };


4. Install dependencies:
bash
npm install --save-dev @types/vscode

5. Press F5 to test the extension
6. Use `Ctrl+Shift+D` (or `Cmd+Shift+D` on Mac) to insert the date"
==== install locally:
7.
npm install
npm install -g @vscode/vsce
vsce package //create .vsix file
8.
in VSCode-palette: Extensions: Install from VSIX
find .vsix file

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-25},

"General
Ctrl+Shift+P, F1 Show Command Palette
Ctrl+P Quick Open, Go to File…
Ctrl+Shift+N New window/instance
Ctrl+Shift+W Close window/instance
Ctrl+, User Settings
Ctrl+K Ctrl+S Keyboard Shortcuts
---
Basic editing
Ctrl+X Cut line (empty selection)
Ctrl+C Copy line (empty selection)
Alt+ ↑ / ↓ Move line up/down
Shift+Alt + ↓ / ↑ Copy line up/down
Ctrl+Shift+K Delete line
Ctrl+Enter Insert line below
Ctrl+Shift+Enter Insert line above
Ctrl+Shift+\ Jump to matching bracket
Ctrl+] / [ Indent/outdent line
Home / End Go to beginning/end of line
Ctrl+Home Go to beginning of file
Ctrl+End Go to end of file

keyboard-rule of VscCmds

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-15},
× https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/configure/keybindings#_keyboard-rules,
× https://code.visualstudio.com/api/references/when-clause-contexts,
"A keyboard shortcut configuration in VS Code is also known as a keyboard rule. Each rule consists of the following attributes:
* key: describes the pressed key(s), for example Ctrl+F.
* command: the identifier of the VS Code command to execute, for example workbench.view.explorer to open the Explorer view.
* when: (optional) clause containing a boolean expression that is evaluated depending on the current context."
[{2026-04-15 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/configure/keybindings#_keyboard-rules]

name::
* McsEngl.VscCmds'keyboard-rule,
* McsEngl.Vscode'keyboard-rule,

keybindings.json of VscCmds

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-15},

How to add a keyboard shortcut to an action, for example, add Ctrl+D to Delete Lines
Find a rule that triggers the action in the Default Keyboard Shortcuts and write a modified version of it in your keybindings.json file:
// Original, in Default Keyboard Shortcuts
{ "key": "ctrl+shift+k", "command": "editor.action.deleteLines", "when": "editorTextFocus" },
// Modified, in User/keybindings.json, Ctrl+D now will also trigger this action
{ "key": "ctrl+d", "command": "editor.action.deleteLines", "when": "editorTextFocus" },

* McsEngl.VscCmd.Preferences: Open Keyboard Shortcuts (JSON),

"Here are the most useful **VS Code file commands**.
For **Windows/Linux**:
* `Ctrl+N` — New file
* `Ctrl+O` — Open file
* `Ctrl+S` — Save
* `Ctrl+Shift+S` — Save As
* `Ctrl+K S` — Save All
* `Ctrl+W` or `Ctrl+F4` — Close file
* `Ctrl+K Ctrl+W` — Close all files
* `Ctrl+Shift+T` — Reopen closed file/editor
* `Ctrl+P` — Quick Open file by name
* `Ctrl+K P` — Copy active file path
* `Ctrl+K R` — Reveal active file in Explorer
* `Ctrl+K O` — Open active file in new window
* `Ctrl+K Ctrl+O` — Open folder
* `Ctrl+Shift+E` — Focus Explorer
* `Ctrl+Alt+F` — Filter files/folders in Explorer
* `Ctrl+click` on a file path — Open linked file, or create it from a path in some contexts ([Visual Studio Code][1])
For **macOS**:
* `⌘N` — New file
* `⌘O` — Open file
* `⌘S` — Save
* `⇧⌘S` — Save As
* `⌥⌘S` — Save All
* `⌘W` — Close file
* `⌘K ⌘W` — Close all files
* `⇧⌘T` — Reopen closed file/editor
* `⌘P` — Quick Open file by name
* `⌘K P` — Copy active file path
* `⌘K R` — Reveal active file in Finder
* `⌘K O` — Open active file in new window
* `⇧⌘E` — Focus Explorer ([Visual Studio Code][2])
A few very handy **Command Palette** commands:
* `File: New File`
* `File: Open File`
* `File: Save`
* `File: Save All`
* `File: Revert File`
* `File: Copy Path of Active File`
* `File: Reveal Active File in Explorer`
* `File: Open Folder` ([Visual Studio Code][3])
You can also turn on **Auto Save** from `File > Auto Save`, so files save automatically without pressing save each time. ([Visual Studio Code][4])
If you want, I can also give you a **clean cheat sheet grouped by File / Explorer / Tabs / Folder commands**.

description::

· https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/debugtest/tasks

· Tasks in VS Code can be configured to run scripts and start processes so that many of existing tools [like linting, building, packaging, mostly run from the command line] can be used from within VS Code without having to enter a command line or write new code. Workspace or folder specific tasks are configured from the tasks.json file in the .vscode folder for a workspace.

user-input of VscTask

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-04-18},
Yes, VS Code tasks can absolutely take user input. This is achieved by defining custom `inputs` in your `tasks.json` file and then referencing them in your task definition .
There are two primary types of user input you can use:
* **`promptString`**: For free-form text input. When the task runs, an input box will appear where the user can type any string.
* **`pickString`**: For selecting from a predefined list of options. A dropdown will appear with the options you've specified.
* **Complete Example (`tasks.json`):**
json
{
 "version": "2.0.0",
 "tasks": [
   {
     "label": "Echo User Comment",
     "type": "shell",
     "command": "echo",
     "args": ["User said: ${input:userComment}"],
     "problemMatcher": []
   },
   {
     "label": "Deploy to Environment",
     "type": "shell",
     "command": "npm",
     "args": ["run", "deploy", "${input:deployEnv}"],
     "problemMatcher": []
   }
 ],
 "inputs": [
   {
     "id": "userComment",
     "type": "promptString",
     "description": "Enter a comment",
     "default": "No comment"
   },
   {
     "id": "deployEnv",
     "type": "pickString",
     "description": "Select the deployment environment",
     "options": ["development", "staging", "production"],
     "default": "development"
   }
 ]
}
When you run the "Echo User Comment" task, a dialog will pop up asking you to "Enter a comment". Your input will then be passed to the `echo` command. The "Deploy to Environment" task will present a dropdown with the three environment options .
### Important Note on Empty Input
There is a known issue in some versions of VS Code where providing an **empty string** for a `promptString` input can cause the task to fail silently. As a workaround, ensure you enter at least one character, or provide a `default` value so the input is never truly empty when the user just presses Enter .
[{2026-04-18 retrieved} https://chat.deepseek.com/a/chat/s/527c9e58-7c73-4be6-bd29-d9261347dc69]

VscCmd.Snippets//preferences//file

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-28},
× https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/editing/userdefinedsnippets,
"VS Code snippets are templates that make it easier to enter repeating code patterns, such as loops or conditional-statements. They can be triggered by typing a "prefix" and pressing `Tab`, or through the **IntelliSense** menu.
---
## 1. Types of Snippets
VS Code categorizes snippets based on their "scope" (where they are available):
* **Language-Specific:** Only appear when you are editing a specific file type (e.g., `.js`, `.py`).
* **Global Snippets:** Available across all file types. These files use the `.code-snippets` extension.
* **Project-Specific:** Saved in a `.vscode` folder at the root of your workspace. These are perfect for sharing specific team patterns via Git.
* **Extension Snippets:** Many extensions (like "ES7+ React/Redux/React-Native snippets") bundle their own snippets into the editor.
---
## 2. Anatomy of a Snippet
Snippets are defined in **JSON** files. Here is a breakdown of the standard structure:
json
"Print to console": {
"prefix": "log",
"body": [
"console.log('${1:message}');",
"$0"
],
"description": "Log output to console"
}

### Key Components:
* **Prefix:** The text you type to trigger the snippet (e.g., typing `log`).
* **Body:** The actual code to insert. It can be a single string or an array of strings (each element is a new line).
* **Description:** Hover text shown in the IntelliSense menu.
* **Tab Stops (`$1`, `$2`):** Defines where the cursor moves when you press `Tab`.
* **Placeholders (`${1:default}`):** Tab stops with default text that is highlighted for easy replacement.
* **Final Cursor Position (`$0`):** Where the cursor ends up after you finish tabbing through the snippet.
---
## 3. Dynamic Snippets (Variables)
You can use built-in variables to make snippets "smarter."
| Variable | Description |
| `TM_FILENAME` | The name of the current file. |
| `TM_DIRECTORY` | The path to the current directory. |
| `CURRENT_YEAR` | The current year (e.g., 2026). |
| `CLIPBOARD` | The contents of your clipboard. |
| `TM_SELECTED_TEXT` | The text you had selected before triggering the snippet. |
---
## 4. How to Manage Snippets
To create or edit your own, follow these steps:
1. Open the **Command Palette** (`Ctrl+Shift+P` or `Cmd+Shift+P`).
2. Type **"Snippets: Configure User Snippets"**.
3. Select a language (e.g., `python`) or choose **"New Global Snippets file"**.
4. Add your JSON definition inside the curly braces of the file that opens.
---
## 5. Pro Tips
* **Choice Menus:** You can provide a list of options using `${1|choice1,choice2,choice3|}`.
* **Transformation:** You can use RegEx to transform variables (e.g., turning a filename into a ClassName by capitalizing the first letter).
* **Tab Completion:** If snippets aren't appearing when you hit Tab, ensure `"editor.tabCompletion": "on"` is set in your `settings.json`.
**Would you like me to help you write a specific custom snippet for a language you're currently using?**"
[{2026-03-28 retrieved} https://gemini.google.com/app/b70993dde329cb00]

"No. actions.find is the plain “open in-editor Find” command, and the current VS Code built-in commands reference does not list any arguments for it. Recent VS Code issues also confirm that passing args to actions.find does not work.
If you want arguments, use editor.actions.findWithArgs instead. VS Code documents these supported fields: searchString, replaceString, isRegex, preserveCase, findInSelection, matchWholeWord, and isCaseSensitive." [ChatGpt]

View/Command Palette...: Ctrl+Shift+P
· From here, you have access to all functionality within VS Code, including keyboard shortcuts for the most common operations.
· The Command Palette supports different modes of operation:
1) Command mode (>): after the > symbol, start typing to filter the command list. For example, type move terminal to find commands to move the terminal to a new window.
2) Quick Open mode: remove the > character and start typing to search for files in your workspace. You can use the Ctrl+P keyboard shortcut to open the Command Palette and start searching for files directly.
3) Symbol search mode (#): replace the > character by # to search for symbols like variables or functions in your code.
[{2026-03-26 retrieved} https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/getstarted/getting-started]

VscCmd.internal

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-28},
"Visual Studio Code relies on a robust "Command" system. Every action you take—from saving a file to splitting the editor—is mapped to an internal command ID (e.g., `workbench.action.files.save`).
Here is how to access, use, and identify these internal commands.
---
## 1. How to Find Any Command ID
If you want to use a command in a script, keybinding, or extension, you first need its internal ID.
* **Keyboard Shortcuts Editor:** Press `Ctrl+K Ctrl+S` (Windows/Linux) or `Cmd+K Cmd+S` (Mac). Right-click any command and select **Copy Command ID**.
* **Default Keybindings JSON:** To see the "master list" including those without shortcuts, open the Command Palette and run: `Preferences: Open Default Keyboard Shortcuts (JSON)`.
* **Developer Tools:** If you want to see what command is triggered when you click a button, open the Developer Tools (`Help > Toggle Developer Tools`) and watch the Console/Network tab (though this is more for core contributors).
---
## 2. Common Internal Command Categories
Most internal commands follow a specific naming convention based on their scope:
### Editor Actions (`editor.action.*`)
These control the text within the active window.
| Command ID | Action |
| `editor.action.formatDocument` | Formats the current file. |
| `editor.action.commentLine` | Toggles a line comment. |
| `editor.action.rename` | Triggers "Rename Symbol" (F2). |
| `editor.action.clipboardCopyAction` | Standard copy to clipboard. |
### Workbench Actions (`workbench.action.*`)
These control the UI, layout, and global features.
| Command ID | Action |
| `workbench.action.files.save` | Saves the current file. |
| `workbench.action.terminal.toggleTerminal` | Shows/hides the integrated terminal. |
| `workbench.action.splitEditor` | Splits the editor into two groups. |
| `workbench.action.reloadWindow` | Restarts the VS Code UI. |
---
## 3. Advanced Navigation Prefixes
The **Command Palette** (`Ctrl+Shift+P`) is actually a multi-tool. You can change what it searches for by using these internal prefixes:
| Prefix | Mode | Description |
| `>` | **Command Mode** | Run any VS Code or Extension command. |
| `@` | **Symbol Search** | Find variables, functions, or classes in the *current* file. |
| `#` | **Workspace Symbol** | Search for symbols across the *entire* project. |
| `:` | **Go to Line** | Type a number to jump to that line. |
| `?` | **Help** | Displays all available palette prefixes. |
---
## 4. Running Commands Programmatically
If you are building an extension or a task, you can trigger these commands using the API:
javascript
// Example: Saving all files via the API
vscode.commands.executeCommand('workbench.action.files.saveAll');

**Note on "Internal" Commands:** Commands starting with an underscore (e.g., `_executeCodeActionProvider`) are considered private APIs. They often require specific arguments and are subject to change, so use them with caution.
---
**Would you like me to show you how to map one of these internal commands to a custom keybinding in your `keybindings.json`?**"

VscCmd.shell

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2026-03-28},
"Here's a comprehensive guide to VS Code shell commands:
## Installing VS Code Shell Commands
If you want to use `code` command in terminal:
### macOS
- Open VS Code
- Open Command Palette (`Cmd+Shift+P`)
- Type "Shell Command: Install 'code' command in PATH"
- Click to install
### Windows
- During installation, check "Add to PATH" option
- Or manually add VS Code's `bin` folder to PATH
### Linux
- Usually installed automatically with package managers
- Or create symlink: `sudo ln -s /path/to/vscode/bin/code /usr/local/bin/code`
## Basic Shell Commands
bash
# Open current directory in VS Code
code .
# Open specific file
code filename.txt
# Open specific folder
code /path/to/folder
# Open multiple files
code file1.txt file2.txt
# Open in new window
code -n
# Open in existing window (reuse current)
code -r
# Compare two files (diff)
code --diff file1.txt file2.txt
# Open with specific line and column
code -g file.txt:10:5

## Useful Command Line Options
bash
# List all options
code --help
# Disable extensions temporarily
code --disable-extensions
# Open with specific profile
code --profile "Profile Name"
# Open in safe mode (extensions disabled)
code --safe
# Wait for files to close before returning
code --wait
# Show verbose output
code --verbose

## Git Integration
bash
# Use VS Code as git editor
git config --global core.editor "code --wait"
# Use VS Code as git diff tool
git config --global diff.tool vscode
git config --global difftool.vscode.cmd 'code --wait --diff $LOCAL $REMOTE'
# View git diff in VS Code
git difftool

## Aliases (Optional)
Add to `~/.bashrc`, `~/.zshrc`, or `~/.config/fish/config.fish`:
bash
# Common aliases
alias c="code"
alias c.="code ."
alias cn="code -n"
alias cdiff="code --diff"

## Platform-Specific Notes
### Windows (PowerShell/Cmd)
powershell
# Similar commands work
code .
code file.txt
# Using with PowerShell
code . -r # Reuse window

### WSL (Windows Subsystem for Linux)
bash
# Install VS Code server in WSL
code .
# This automatically installs required components

## Troubleshooting
If `code` command isn't recognized:
1. Restart terminal after installation
2. Check PATH: `echo $PATH` (macOS/Linux) or `echo %PATH%` (Windows)
3. Reinstall shell command from VS Code Command Palette
Would you like more details about any specific shell command or scenario?"

relation-to-Atom of Vscode

description::
× HitpMcs-creation: {2025-12-11},
"Below is a clear, business-style explanation of the relationship between **Atom** and **Visual Studio Code (VS Code)**, including their history, shared technology layers, and ecosystem implications.
---
## 1. High-Level Relationship
**Atom and VS Code are unrelated products built by different companies**, but they are connected indirectly because **both were built on top of the same underlying framework: Electron**.
* **Atom** was developed by **GitHub** (launched 2014).
* **VS Code** was developed by **Microsoft** (launched 2015).
* Both applications run on **Electron**, a framework originally created by GitHub to build desktop apps with web technologies.
---
## 2. Shared Foundation: Electron
Atom was the **first major application** built on the original Electron codebase (then called Atom Shell).
Microsoft later adopted Electron to build VS Code.
This creates the appearance of a relationship, but the editors themselves were developed entirely separately.
Key implications:
* Similar UI technology stack (HTML, CSS, JavaScript).
* Cross-platform behavior is consistent across both.
* Extensions in both systems often leveraged Node.js packages.
---
## 3. Development Philosophy Differences
Although they share a foundation, their **editor philosophies diverged significantly**.
### **Atom**
* Designed as a fully hackable, modular text editor.
* Deep customization through plugins and user configuration.
* Performance was sometimes criticized due to heavy customization layers.
### **VS Code**
* Focused on performance, developer productivity, and integrations.
* Strong built-in tooling for debugging, terminals, Git, and language servers.
* Opinionated but extensible marketplace.
---
## 4. Acquisition and Strategic Shift
In **2018**, Microsoft acquired **GitHub**.
After the acquisition:
* Microsoft continued investing in VS Code, not Atom.
* Atom’s development slowed.
* In **June 2022**, GitHub officially discontinued Atom and its associated packages.
Because Microsoft already owned VS Code, continuing Atom did not make strategic sense.
---
## 5. Impact on Users and Ecosystem
* **Atom is deprecated**, no longer maintained.
* Many Atom users migrated to **VS Code**, which offered better performance and a larger extension library.
* Some community-driven Atom forks (e.g., Pulsar) emerged to keep the ecosystem alive.
---
## Summary
| Aspect | Atom | VS Code |
| Origin | GitHub | Microsoft |
| Year released | 2014 | 2015 |
| Technology | Electron | Electron |
| Philosophy | Hackable editor | Fast, productivity-focused IDE-like editor |
| Status | Discontinued (2022) | Actively maintained |
**Relationship:**
Atom and VS Code share a common technical foundation (Electron), but they were developed independently and diverged in performance, adoption, and strategic relevance. Microsoft’s acquisition of GitHub ended Atom’s development in favor of VS Code.
---
If you want, I can also provide:
* A short historical timeline.
* A comparison of technical architectures.
* A migration guide from Atom packages/workflows to VS Code equivalents."

name::

evoluting of Vscode

description::
"VS Code evolved in **three big jumps**:
1. **Editor (2015–2016)**
Microsoft announced Visual Studio Code at Build 2015 as a **cross-platform** code editor, then shipped **VS Code 1.0** in April 2016. Its roots were the browser-based **Monaco** editor, wrapped in **Electron** with Node.js so it could feel native while staying highly portable. ([Microsoft for Developers][1])
2. **Platform (2016–2018)**
It quickly became more than “just an editor” because the **extension model** was central to the product. VS Code’s API let authors add languages, debuggers, UI pieces, and workflows, and even many core capabilities were built as extensions using the same API. ([Visual Studio Code][2])
3. **Workspace anywhere (2019–2022)**
A major shift came with **Remote Development** in 2019: Containers, SSH, and WSL let developers keep the UI local while code and tooling lived elsewhere. Then VS Code expanded to **Notebooks** in 2020 and to the browser with **vscode.dev** in 2021. By 2022 it had matured with features like **Profiles**, the **merge editor**, and **extension signing** for marketplace security. ([Visual Studio Code][3])
Then came the current phase:
4. **AI-native IDE (2023–2026)**
In 2023 VS Code began deeply integrating **GitHub Copilot** into the editor experience, including chat and inline editing. In 2024 it exposed **Chat and Language Model APIs** so extension authors could build AI features into VS Code itself. In 2025 Microsoft said it would open source the Copilot Chat extension as part of making VS Code an **“open source AI editor.”** By 2026, the product message had shifted further to **agentic / multi-agent development**, with local, background, and cloud agents built into the workflow. ([Visual Studio Code][4])
So the simplest way to describe the evolution is:
**text editor → extensible developer platform → remote/cloud workspace → AI and agent development hub**. The current official release line already shows this shift clearly: as of **April 22, 2026**, VS Code is at **version 1.118**, and its homepage/docs emphasize AI agents and multi-agent development. ([Visual Studio Code][5])
I can turn this into a **year-by-year timeline** next if you want.
